There's so many different types of cornbread that would have been acceptable as a representative of traditional cornbread. There's plain Jane from the box's instructions, which seeks to please everyone, but impresses a very few. Then there is the plain Jane that is sweet. It is still decent enough to keep itself alive as a recipe, but really out of place when it is too sweet.  Then there are the family variations that would substitute buttermilk, for the whole milk, and add creamed corn and sweet corn in to the mix. This would be a fair representation of corn bread made by people trying to please.  Then you would have the Texas varieties, and here are a few, you'd have the creamed corn and buttermilk, but also cheddar cheese, diced onion, diced red bell peppers, and pickled jalapeÃ±os.  Then you'd have a chili cornbread, made with the buttermilk, the cheddar cheese, and chili powder added, which is Ancho peppers, cumin, cayenne peppers, onion powder, garlic powder, paprika, black pepper, & salt.  That ancho pepper and cumin are the mysterious flavors many find hard to describe, and they are the signature components to the flavoring of canned Chili, chili recipes.

This video made me really desperately want BBQ and for that reason I'm sad (just moved, don't know the good places yet.) So instead I'm going to spend my BBQ energy in the form of ranting BBQ-ly into the comments. Please be aware, fellow commenters, that I've never even stepped foot in Texas so this is just more general BBQ knowledge.  I used to be 100% all in on doing the BBQ sandwich, just some pulled pork, brisket, slaw, and sauce on garlic bread (which is different from just ordering a pulled pork sandwich. There's a kind of satisfaction in assembling it yourself.) But recently my absolute favorite thing is just... get my side of baked beans, tear up some meat into it, and mix it in with sauce. Delicious every time.  Something that interests me is the fact that as soon as we started leaving FL, every BBQ place we saw started offering potato salad... and it's become my favorite BBQ side. The thing about coleslaw is that some people do it amazingly, but I absolutely HATE it when it just tastes like vinegar. Gotta be primarily mayo - and potato salad is almost always mayo. It's honestly kind of genius because everywhere I went to before moving, coleslaw was the only light and creamy side, which I feel is really necessary when you've got either rich greasy meat or bread/cornbread. Potato salad lets you have that even if you don't like the slaw.  Cornbread is very hit or miss for me, personally. I definitely understand the reactions to it. At worst it's just sort of sand-like in texture (gritty) and flavor (not much). I love the sweet cornbreads, especially the ones with actual corn bits in em. As far as BBQ breads go, though, IMO a bun of some kind (specifically, not texas toast) or garlic bread will always win. (Though plain texas toast comes 3rd.)  Brisket is the best BBQ meat. Period. No argument. In those "pick 3 meat" options BBQ places almost always seem to have, I usually get sliced brisket, pulled pork, and sausage of some kind if they have it. I've unfortunately never had good BBQ ribs - and they've been pretty much always pork ribs. Too dry everywhere I've tried them, don't fall off the bone nicely. I need to fix this as soon as possible because those beef ribs looked amazing. I'm also surprised at the lack of smoked chicken, which was always a thing where I grew up (though I am thinking I might've stopped seeing it on menus pretty soon after we left the state... but I'm not a huge fan so I didn't really check.)  Sauce wasn't discussed but I feel like I should stake (steak?) my sauce claim anyway. I'm a big fan of sweet + a lil spicy BBQ. Not too vinegary, not mustard-based or white BBQ sauce. Honestly, if I could use BBQ baked bean sauce as a BBQ sauce I absolutely would.  Also surprised at no dessert! Banana pudding with nilla wafers or cobbler was at pretty much every BBQ place I went to growing up (and at Central BBQ in Memphis, who had the most amazing banana pudding I've ever had? I almost wish we'd stopped driving there and just stayed lol.)  ...You know, this didn't actually help the fact that I really want some BBQ. I don't know why I thought it would. Oh well.

These boys are really being polite and controlled.  When my sister's three teen boys started in on a platter of food it was like a pride of lions sharing a gazelle.  Their was no politeness and it was a feeding frenzy.  That cornbread looked really dry and it can easily happen.  I add some honey to the batter for flavor and when the cornbread is done baking I use a fork to put some holes in the cornbread and then pour melted butter on top and along the sides where the cornbread has pulled away from the baking dish.  The holes allow the cornbread to drink in the butter.  Once the butter is soaked up, I give it a second treatment.  You end up with a moist, not soggy, cornbread that has a honey butter flavor.  Sometimes, on the next day I will cut a couple squares of cornbread and give it a light toasting.  Then I will pour some maple syrup over it.  Have some bacon and\or sausages with it and you've got a delicious breakfast.

To answer the question as to why you guys in the UK don't have barbecued brisket over there is mainly because you guys don't smoke your meats. Or rather I should clarify, none of you besides the few American BBQ restaurants over there, dedicate the time to smoke brisket. Your use of brisket is in pot roasts, stews, and in making beef pastrami.  Personal preference and just a tip for the boys, if you're not a fan of the mac 'n cheese, take the pulled pork, mix some BBQ sauce around in it and then mix that saucy pork into the mac 'n cheese. So are Brits raised from a young age to cover their mouth when chewing regardless of whether their mouth is open or closed? All these video's show people covering their mouths even when chewing? I suppose it's part of a cultural norm, whereas, in the USA, it's mainly reserved for situations where your mouth is full and you are trying to talk AND CHEW at the same time (so as not to show the contents of your mouth to whoever you are talking to). You need honey butter with cornbread.
That cornbread looked dry. I'll bless you all with the best cornbread recipe. Get a 9x9 pan and put a sick of butter in it and put it in a 425 degree oven to melt. While the butter is melting mix 2 cups Martha White self rising cornmeal, 1 beaten egg, 1 1/3 cups of milk or buttermilk and a healthy dollop of sour cream or mayonnaise. Take the pan out of the oven when the butter is melted and pour it in with the cornmeal mix and mix it up good. Then pour back into the pan and back in the oven for about 25 min or until golden brown. You're welcome.

Baked beans have their origins in precolonial New England. White beans (usually navy beans or great northern beans) were slow baked with maple sugar, mustard and/or spices, and a strip of fatty bear meat. The fatty bear meat was eventually replaced with salt pork. In the early part of the last century, Pittsburgh's H.J. Heinz Company decided to market its beans in the UK and began manufacturing them there, at some point the sauce was changed to a tomato based sauce (because much of their other products contained tomato), similar to SpaghettiOs or Chef Boyardee canned pasta. It's not uncommon for people in the Northeastern United States and Maritime Canada to make their own baked beans for holidays, etc. It's an all day affair, but the end result is usually well worth it.

Kentucky here. That is not real Southern cornbread! Real is thicker, unsweet, and less cake like. Also, not usually served with Barbecue. Most of us use Garlic Toast. NEXT, that Sweet tea isn't right! You gave them boys WEAK TEA! No way! Real iced tea is brewed nearly mahogany. This is so to allow the ice to melt without turning it into wash water. Common recipe is a gallon (or so) water in a bucket on the stove, turn it to high heat. Add just about a tea bag for every two cups of water, or three. Yes, add it to the water before it boils. When it boils, turn it off and let it steep about 15 min, or good and dark. Take out tea, squeeze the dickins out of them into pot. Add a cup (or so) of sugar for every gallon of tea. Might let it cool a bit. Pour over a nearly full glass of ice, serve. I abominate lemon in tea, but if you like it, go ahead.(No accounting for taste, said the old lady when she kissed the cow).

That cornbread looked very dry.  Traditionally made with a buttermilk to help not be dry plus before baking gets poured into sizzling hot skillet or pan to form a sort of crust to keep in moisture (like searing a roast or steak).    In the U.S. south, not sweetened but at diner's discretion can be topped with butter, honey or more buttermilk after serving.  When corn is in season, often corn, corn milk, creamed corn or whatever corn leftovers you have can be added.  In the north, they do corn "muffins"  versus "bread" -- they add sugar, syrup, or honey and often bake in regular cake pans or muffin tins without the sizzled crust.  In the southwest, don't be surprised to find hatch ("New Mexico") chiles or jalapeÃ±os added along with whatever corn or salsa fixings you had leftover from other kitchen adventures.  Generally not sweetened but I've seen that change if hotter peppers are used or oddly when served with spicier BBQ meats.

So much more to BBQ than this  as a non Texan Okahoman I don't ever eat cornbread with BBQ  Most  people around here pick a slice of white or wheat bread side,  and  usually a version of  cold potato salad or  mac and cheese  or french fries    coleslaw,  sometimes a slice of raw onion or a pickled jalapeno  (and my favorite side beans have no meat  -baked northern white beans cooked  with hickory BBQ sauce, a tablespoon of honey or molasses  or maple syrup ,and a few drops of Louisiana Hot Sauce . )  The meat is cooked the same way as the meat you tried, but hickory wood is used over mesquite wood the farther east you go. The farther SE you go, the BBQ sauce uses  vinegar and mustard instead of a tomato sauce base.

When I lived in Scotland from 1979-87, our neighbors didn't even know what BBQ was. We used to have massive parties in the summer. Dad would grill burgers and hot dogs. To them a burger was literally ground up pork ham turned into a pattie and served on a super soft bun. Now they all grill outside in all weather and they are now learning about southern smoked BBQ. They will never be the same again.  America's education system should also take note of these kid's ages, the grades they are in and their massive vocabulary. American kids this same age couldn't tell you what most of their big words mean. When we moved back her in 1987, I was 4 years above my grade. It totally sucked because my parents wouldn't allow me to test out and I was so bored. I wasted so many opportunities to shine in school to show my classmates how lacking their education was, but hind sight is always 20//20.

It's worth noting to our UK friends that much of that platter isn't exclusive to either BBQ or Texas. Then best Brisket is usually done by Jewish deli's in New York, cornbread is an almost everywhere thing, ditto macaroni and cheese. Ribs are the same, an everywhere thing, but Texas is known for them more for the quantity and quality as a cattle state. You can find the same in Alberta Canada because they are a cattle province, you get the best where there is a large fresh local supply. But Texas, along with like Tennessee, stand out for their rubs and sauces. So just know that if you come visit north america you will be able to find quality BBQ in many places in both the US and Canada. But a cattle town will always have something a little special.

13:00  As a born and raised Texan, I can 100% agree that is NOT southern tea.   Southern tea is a black tea and very dark.   That is crazy light colored and massively watered down. For my family we all share in our sugar preference and will pre sweeten, but for guests we always give sugar and sugar substitute on the side with unsweetened black tea.   And sometimes we even drink in straight without sweeteners.   But always very dark.   Ours sets in the fridge overnight and is restirred the next morning.

As to cornbread, it depends upon how you make it.  The real traditional way is cup flour, cup corn meal, 3-4 tsp baking powder, egg, 1/4 tsp salt,  1/4 cup oil, 1/2 cup milk.  NO SUGAR.  If you want it the way everyone else outside of the actual South makes it, you have more flour and add sugar.  (Southerners would call it a corn cake, as it has softer texture and is sweet.)  You eat it with butter, and possibly honey, but mainly to add to beans, and other foods.  As to "tea".  You should have said iced sweet tea, not tea.  It's flavor really depends upon the type of tea being used.  (Tetley or Luzzian preferred.)

This makes me miss Texas! I still live in the American South, but each region has their own spin on barbecue. Texas BBQ draws upon the rich history of German (and Czech) immigrants in Texas adapting their meat cooking techniques to the ingredients that were available. Beef instead of pork, corn instead of wheat. But the spice blends (and most importantly the mustard) rubbed onto beef brisket before smoking overnight draw upon German immigrants to Central Texas.

You too can have Texas BBQ in the UK.  Just weld up a smoker with an offset firebox and dry rub a brisket with salt and pepper.  Use a hardwood of your choice.  Keep the temp around 275-300f.  Smoke it unwrapped and don't lift the door for at least 3 hours.  Wrap it and bring it up to at least 205f internal temp and don't take it out until it is floppy.  Pull it and let it rest for at least 45 min.  You're welcome.

I was introduced to brisket when I was in a living history group in Texas.  We went to another historic site and they fed us BBQ.  Brisket, beans, sausage, and corn bread.  Fell in love with the brisket.  Another time we arrived much earlier, and I got to see how they prepared it.  They were using a smoker that was the size of an old fashioned locomotive boiler, and were carefully arranging the briskets to allow the smoke to circulate.  I believe they were using mesquite, but that is not required.  After I found out how uncomplicated it was, I started trying it at home, downsizing from the industrial size smoker.  It is delicious.  The trick is cooking low and slow, which requires keeping a watch on the fire.

Mates! Yâ€™all need to have the cornbread with beans! Especially slow cooked pinto beans. You get the bean liquid like a soup almost, with some butter. Use the cornbread to dip in it. Some folks crumble it into it. I personally like those pinto beans cooked with an onion, garlic and sometimes a jalapeÃ±o or two. Add some herbs and spices if you wish. A bit of thyme, bay leaf. Definitely black pepper and salt. Bbq baked beans (even from a tin) are also nice with cornbread. Charro beans are nice. Cilantro is a nice touch for charro and pinto beans at the end. Cornbread can be sweet or savory. Itâ€™s very easy and fast to make. Please, I encourage you to experiment with this southern American staple and find a recipe you like. You can even make it in a cast iron pan. And you can make actually make it sweet enough to be a desert with some butter cream frosting, jams, or with fresh fruit and a little maple syrup drizzled on it with some warm, melty butter. There are many versions of cornbread so please keep trying it until you find your favorites. Oh, and cheddar and jalapeÃ±o corn bread is also excellent! Cheers!

They are two separate muscles in the brisket, grain running in different directions, which is why you cut a brisket in half,  slice the thinner side across the brisket, and slice the thicker side along the brisket. As a Texan, my biggest complaint about nearly every BBQ joint is that they will invest 12-18 hours into slow smoking their brisket and they won't invest the 6 hours to make next level pinto beans.  Brisket has to be pretty heavily trimmed for smoking.  That abundance of trim is why BBQ joints will make their own sausages to smoke alongside the brisket.  But because professional trimming yields far more fat than meat in the trim, sausage making still leaves a ton of unrenderable beef fat unused given the optimal meat to fat ratios for sausage.  The piles of surplus beef fat can then be cut up and rendered down into beef tallow on its own.  This allows for wrapping briskets in tallow lined butcher paper when it comes off of the smoker and goes into a warmer to rest for another 12 hours or so at like 150-160 degree Fahrenheit (of 65-70 degrees Celsius).  When you do a very low 6 hour slow simmer of pinto beans with a healthy dose of beef tallow in the water, the starch released by the beans as they absorb warm water over several hour mixes with and interacts with the beef fat and what should be a deliberate mix of seasonings to generate a slightly gelatinous, opaque and incredibly flavorful bean broth that coats the beans.  Especially, given the degree to which the British put their beans on toast (or bread), I would expect them to appreciate wat the difference in method adds.  Doing all of this very slowly at a 6 hour pace as opposed to a 2 hour pace makes all the difference in the world.  Admittedly, the use of black pepper, crushed red pepper flakes, cayenne, granulated garlic, onion powder and salt might be overwhelming for a British palate.  ğŸ˜œ

That was TEXAS Barbecue, NOT Southern barbecue. In the South you have vinegar based, ketchup based, mayonnaise based, as well as mustard based sauces, you have pork shoulder, ribs, ham, whole hog, chicken, sausages, beef and even mutton/goat barbecue, some venison.  While Texas barbecue is prepared using the same techniques, the dry rub coating of salt and black pepper as well as the type of wood effects everything.     The wood burning emparts flavor that impacts the taste. Nortb Carolina mainly uses Hickory wood, Texas unless importing Hickory uses Oak and  Mesquite along with fruit woods like Apple or Cherry.  Unless you brought over a Southern cook the cornbread you served probably wasn't "real" cornbread.  Even in the South, cornbread can create arguments. The color of the cornmeal used, type of fat as well as liquid, adding sugar, or kennels of corn, adding jalapeÃ±os  or what is known as cracklings to the batter can create drama.    Cracklings are the  remains of rendering lard from pork fat.  Cut the non-cured pork fat into cubes between quarter and half inch thick.  Place cubes into pot with water that you heat to a simmer. As the water heats the lard liquefies and will float on top of the water and the flesh which held the fat will fry leaving tiny chunks of porky goodness that some with add the cornbread or corn muffins for a burst of flavor. Similar to pork skins but not exact.    Cornbread is usually served with butter, honey and leftovers are used crumbled into a bowl with either milk or buttermilk poured on for breakfast or snack.  Was common for both of my parents to do the next morning or even later that night.

We use 8 little tea bags for a gallon, but you can use 8-16. If we use a big tea bag we add a coupl of little ones. Elder Texan here.  (mid-50s)  Sweet tea is an abomination.  From what I gather it infiltrated Texas from southern restaurant chains like PoFolks, Grandys, and Chick-Fil-A.  Through even the 80s in the Greater Houston area, restaurants did not serve sweet tea.  Do I hate it, no.  The problem is that it is not a thirst quencher.  The more I drink, the thirstier I get. American here ... To be fair - not all cornbreads are created equal. I don't particularly like regular (plainly made) cornbread for this very reason - it's often too dry or gritty, or too sticky & sweet (some add extra creamed corn to their plain recipe, which may or may not have added sugar in it). The dry cornbreads are better enjoyed topped with extra butter and honey - it's the only way to save it, and it's delicious - but then it's like entering sweet bread/cake territory, and if you're not in the mood for sweet, it doesn't register as delicious.  The best cornbreads walk the savory-sweet line, by adding extra whole corn, cheese, sometimes jalapenos, or even a minced meat and sauce mixture (canned Manwhich is a cheap easy add-in for midwesterners). The extra flavors contrast and highlight the cornbread flavor itself, making it more delicious that way... Or I'm just picky! I don't knowğŸ˜…...but I get it, though, about not liking plain cornbread right off the bat. It's usually better as a platform to dump some chili beans on, and eat it that way!
